0142429112|5.0|Think of these elements:  Oxford's Bodleian Library; a powerful yet inexperienced witch who is a respected scholar with a specialty in the history of alchemy; an ancient and handsome vampire who is the leader of an order of knights; a spellbound manuscript; and a compelling cast of other witches, vampires and demons, both good and evil.  If done right, these are the perfect ingredients for cooking up a story that is interesting, compelling, and full of complexities.  And, boy, does Deborah Harkness get it right!  I loved reading A Discovery of Witches on a variety of levels.  First, there are the great settings:  Oxford and its environs, a castle in the French countryside, and an upstate New York farmhouse that interacts in none too subtle ways with its inhabitants.  Add to that some wonderful characters.  I like that protagonist Diana Bishop, accomplished and respected in her chosen field, is not quite so confident in all aspects of her life.  I also like the dashing vampire Matthew, who is justifiably uber rich and accomplished, having lived many centuries.  The characters surrounding Diana and Matthew are all fascinating in their own right and help shape the story.    Then there are the layers of social, scientific (okay, quasi-scientific) and historical detail.  As someone who has sat in libraries researching, I fully enjoyed the attention to detail Harkness shows in describing the Bodleian and the process of doing historical research.  I love the role science plays in this book, from Diana and Matthew's separate but equally interesting research areas, to the contemplation of Darwin's theory of evolution in relationship to the development of the three species of creatures.  By the same token, I appreciate the effort she puts into imagining and describing how humans are able to live alongside witches, vampires and demons and how the three types of creatures developed ways over the centuries to coexist peacefully, at least for the most part.  The idea of the Congregation, which is a sort of federation of the three species, is interesting, as well as a clever way to address social issues we humans face in our world.  In the end, this novel is a love story--albeit a long and complex one--and, to like this type of story, it is necessary to feel some sympathy for Diana and Matthew.  Granted, neither is a perfectly consistent or rational being and can be annoying as all get out, but, in the end, that is what I like about them.  It is their personal discovery that ultimately keeps the story moving along.  I know I, for one, cannot wait to read more about them.
0439023513|5.0|***spoilers***<br /><br />After I finish a book or movie that I truly love or hate, I always go to the reviews to see what other people say. The reviews here absolutely SHOCKED me... people asking Collins to rewrite the book?!?!  This will be kind of a review of Mockingjay, but probably also a review of the reviews surrounding the book.  One of the biggest complaints I've read is that Katniss was weak and depressed and not the hero we've come to know and love... but that's the entire point! It would not be logical or believable if she was still that same \\"hero.\\" She's a 17-year-old girl who has now been taken from her family TWICE and forced to participate in the Hunger Games.  Now, upon finally reaching District 13 and the chances of a better life, she finds that in many ways District 13 is no better than the Capital.  People are still tortured, there are strict schedules, she can barely go outside. The leaders are just as violent, just as immoral.  Could you expect a 17-year-old to be able to handle all of this?  What made the book so believable and so powerful for me was that all of the characters displayed real reactions that and evolved in ways that, while maybe not beneficial or admiral, made SENSE.  A story does not have to be written with a person changing for the better, it can be about a persons life falling apart.<br /><br />I loved this book for so many reasons I didn't love Twilight.  Love is NOT the center of the universe, there are more important things going on, and that is clear in Mockingjay.  One of the reasons I hated Twilight was that in the end Bella made no sacrifices and got EVERYTHING. In Mockingjay Katniss made endless sacrifices and ended up physically and emotionally scarred and mentally unstable.<br /><br />In Mockingjay, you have to find the beauty.  It's hidden,  There is a lot of death and destruction, but that's what war is. And there are wars happening right now, people. There are also \\"districts\\" (china/india/nepal) that supply much of our goods while leading terrible lifestyles, with little pay, terrible governments and sex slave trade.  This is a book about real things that really happy that most people in America want to pretend do not exist. He's been living it for years.<br /><br />So, to conclude this rambly mess, you will not like or understand this book if you're not ready for it. We watched as nations pulled tricks to destroy each other, when it turns out they were not nearly as different as presented.  Not one person came out unscathed.<br /><br/>One last thing. It honestly sickens me for people to be \\"demanding a rerewrite.\\" Just because you don't like or understand a book doesn't mean the author needs to rewrite it! Mockingjay was bigger than Katniss and her silly love triangle - it was what happens in war. When people FIGHT to change something UNJUST.... and The Hunger Games were just that! No matter what she does, she's a pawn. She will never be free. In the freedom fight she's not free, and it's not until she's tried and cast as a lunatic and sent back to 12 to live that she begins to rebuild a semblance of a life.<br /><br />This book was real. 1932907009|5.0|At the urging of several members of the Nebraska Writers Workshop, this book was selected for review of screenwriting basics for our calendar of topics in 2012. B004WJREMW|5.0|This is not the starting place for someone unfamiliar with McCartney's solo work. This is a strange, largely experimental album that McCartney recorded entirely on his own. It is self-indulgent, often aimless, and padded with throwaway tracks. That said, as a diehard McCartney fan I have grown to really love this album for the quirky mess it is.   Don't expect a gigantic leap in sonic quality from the original CD release. The somewhat muddy, dull sound must be inherent in the master tapes because this reissue still sounds basically the same. Luckily this hasn't been made a victim of the 'loudness war' and doesn't seem overcompressed at all. There is a bit richer bass end to the remaster that gives it a nice full sound. But the remaster isn't revelatory.  The bonus disc is a great treasure trove of weirdness for fans.  Again, casual fans will probably be perplexed by this mix of oddities.  The remixed \\"Coming Up\\" Live at Glasgow is fine, but unfortunately the original single mix is curiously absent. The 1986 mix - heavily overdubbed - of \\"Blue Sway\\" is a little too smooth sounding. The real gems are the album outtakes. I was surprised to find four of them grouped together as two medleys, but it really isn't a problem. \\"Mr H Atom\\" and \\"All You Horseriders\\" are two of the most bizarre things McCartney has committed to tape (that the public has heard anyway).  \\"Bogey Wobble\\" is a strangely fascinating intstrumental. The original \\"Blue Sway\\" sports a nice, simple funky groove. \\"Check My Machine\\" and \\"Secret Friend\\" are here - they were originally bonus tracks on the first CD of this album.  If you enjoy McCartney's Fireman albums, particularly the most recent \\"Electric Arguments,\\" I highly recommend \\"McCartney II.\\" If you're already a fan of the album, this two disc version is well worth the upgrade for the official release of the oft-bootlegged outtakes. If you're a superfan of the album, splurge on the deluxe version (which adds a DVD with a fascinating 1980 McCartneyinterview, among other tidbits, and also a third CD with unedited, previously unreleased versions of numerous album cuts).

B000VNMMQG|5.0|If you're not into history, Christianity or politics, then this film probably isn't for you.<br />If you are, then this film is pretty much definitive of its class in being the story of how one man can, within his lifetime, achieve something noble and leave the world in a far better place than if he'd never existed.<br /><br />Though it is quite a predictable plot - starting with his friendship with [[ASIN:0007147201 William Pitt the Younger]] and ending with his success in banning the slave trade - the film maker has done his best to take slightly different tangents, such as including hymnwriter John Newton and making his lesser known wife and William Pitt into central charactors in the tale.<br />With these charactors, it is also worth mentioning that the actors chosen are generally first rate, and the script they're given doesn't have any special howlers within it.<br /><br />In being a story that is as well-known as this one, it would be easy to get this film totally wrong. Just imagine a story of Martin Luther King to see how wrong it could go (e.g. doing a 'postmodern perspective' or, conversely, sucking up to his legacy).<br />But as I sit here in the aftermath of the film, it feels like the first time I'd seen [[ASIN:B00003CX95 Braveheart]] - the movie may not be perfect, but the bagpipes linger on, as does the spirit of the film.<br /><br />So even if your normal films include [[ASIN:B002ZG999U The Expendables]] or [[ASIN:B0009UC7H0 Sahara]] (i.e big budget and simple), do give this a go as it is truly a rewarding film, and not just some exercise in evangelism or boring politics. As a non-believer and B-movie junky, I can honestly say that this film is well worth watching, even if its only to make you a better person.<br /><br />P.S. I forgot to mention that the reason I watched this film, was because of this pamphlet: [[ASIN:1905887116 Wilberforce: The Challenge for Today]]. Well worth a read, if you want to know more about Wilberforce and have a spare 30 minutes...
